<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">9th-century mathematician and astronomer</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"al-Khwārizmī" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Al-Khw%C4%81rizm%C4%AB_(disambiguation)" title="Al-Khwārizmī (disambiguation)">al-Khwārizmī (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox biography vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%; font-weight:bold;"><span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">محمد بن موسی خوارزمی</span></span> <span class="languageicon" style="font-size:100%; font-weight:normal">(<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Born</th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="circa">c.</span><span href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Academic background</th><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Academic work</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Era</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> <br/> (<a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid era</a>)</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Main interests</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">Mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Notable works</th><td class="infobox-data"><i><a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing</a></i>, <i>Book of the Description of the Earth, Astronomical tables of Siddhanta</i></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Notable ideas</th><td class="infobox-data">Treatises on <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influenced</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Abu_Kamil" title="Abu Kamil">Abu Kamil</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor-AK_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-AK-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[note 1]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">محمد بن موسی خوارزمی</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Persian" title="Romanization of Persian">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Persian-language romanization"><i lang="fa-Latn">Muḥammad ibn Musā al-Khwārazmi</i></span>; <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 780</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 850</span>), or <b>al-Khwarizmi</b> was a <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> from <a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corbin_1998_44_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corbin_1998_44-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_and_medicine_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_and_medicine-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> who produced vastly influential works in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">astronomy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">geography</a>. Around 820 CE, he was appointed as the astronomer and head of the library of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maher_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maher-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup>
</p><p>Al-Khwarizmi's popularizing treatise on algebra (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing</a></i>, c. 813–833 CE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oaks_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oaks-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 171">: 171 </span></sup>) presented the first systematic solution of <a href="/wiki/Linear_equation" title="Linear equation">linear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_equation" title="Quadratic equation">quadratic equations</a>. One of his principal achievements in algebra was his demonstration of how to solve quadratic equations by <a href="/wiki/Completing_the_square" title="Completing the square">completing the square</a>, for which he provided geometric justifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maher_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maher-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup> Because he was the first to treat algebra as an independent discipline and introduced the methods of "reduction" and "balancing" (the transposition of subtracted terms to the other side of an equation, that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> he has been described as the father<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corbin_1998_44_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corbin_1998_44-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> or founder<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>. The term <i>algebra</i> itself comes from the title of his book (the word <i>al-jabr</i> meaning "completion" or "rejoining").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> His name gave rise to the terms <i><a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">algorism</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">[21]</a></sup> as well as Spanish, Italian and Portuguese terms <i>algoritmo,</i> and Spanish <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/guarismo" title="wikt:guarismo">guarismo</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/algarismo" title="wikt:algarismo">algarismo</a></i> meaning "<a href="/wiki/Numerical_digit" title="Numerical digit">digit</a>".
</p><p>In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> translations of <a href="#Arithmetic">his textbook on arithmetic</a> (<i>Algorithmo de Numero Indorum</i>) which codified the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_numerals" title="Indian numerals">Indian numerals</a>, introduced the <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimal</a> <a href="/wiki/Positional_notation" title="Positional notation">positional number system</a> to the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struik_93_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struik_93-24">[23]</a></sup> <i>The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing</i>, translated into Latin by <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Chester" title="Robert of Chester">Robert of Chester</a> in 1145, was used until the sixteenth century as the principal mathematical text-book of <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_universities" title="List of medieval universities">European universities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to his best-known works, he revised <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>, listing the longitudes and latitudes of various cities and localities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup> He further produced a set of astronomical tables and wrote about calendaric works, as well as the astrolabe and the sundial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> He also made important contributions to <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a>, producing accurate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a> tables, and the first table of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangents" title="Tangents">tangents</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#General_references"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">General references</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Few details of al-Khwārizmī's life are known with certainty. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">Ibn al-Nadim</a> gives his birthplace as <a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a>, and he is generally thought to have come from this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_and_medicine_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Science_and_medicine-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-32">[31]</a></sup> His name means 'the native of <a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a>', a region that was part of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> and is now part of <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari</a> gives his name as Muḥammad ibn Musá al-Khwārizmī al-<a href="/wiki/Majus" title="Majus">Majūsī</a> al-Quṭrubbullī (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">محمد بن موسى الخوارزميّ المجوسـيّ القطربّـليّ</span></span>). The <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> <i>al-Qutrubbulli</i> could indicate he might instead have come from Qutrubbul (Qatrabbul),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> a viticulture district near Baghdad. However, Rashed denies this:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is no need to be an expert on the period or a philologist to see that al-Tabari's second citation should read "Muhammad ibn Mūsa al-Khwārizmī <i>and</i> al-Majūsi al-Qutrubbulli," and that there are two people (al-Khwārizmī and al-Majūsi al-Qutrubbulli) between whom the letter <i>wa</i> [Arabic '<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">و</span></span>' for the conjunction '<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%88#Etymology_2" title="wikt:و">and</a>'] has been omitted in an early copy. This would not be worth mentioning if a series of errors concerning the personality of al-Khwārizmī, occasionally even the origins of his knowledge, had not been made. Recently, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_J._Toomer" title="Gerald J. Toomer">G.J. Toomer</a> ... with naive confidence constructed an entire fantasy on the error which cannot be denied the merit of amusing the reader.</p></blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/David_A._King_(historian)" title="David A. King (historian)">David A. King</a> affirms his <a href="/wiki/Nisba_(onomastics)" title="Nisba (onomastics)">nisba</a> to Qutrubul, noting that he was called al-Khwārizmī al-Qutrubbulli because he was born just outside of Baghdad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding al-Khwārizmī's religion, Toomer writes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-toomer-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Another epithet given to him by al-Ṭabarī, "al-Majūsī," would seem to indicate that he was an adherent of the old <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian religion</a>. This would still have been possible at that time for a man of Iranian origin, but the pious preface to al-Khwārizmī's <i>Algebra</i> shows that he was an orthodox <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>, so al-Ṭabarī's epithet could mean no more than that his forebears, and perhaps he in his youth, had been Zoroastrians.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">Ibn al-Nadīm</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kit%C4%81b_al-Fihrist" title="Kitāb al-Fihrist">Kitāb al-Fihrist</a></i> includes a short biography on al-Khwārizmī together with a list of his books. Al-Khwārizmī accomplished most of his work between 813 and 833. After the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a>, Baghdad had become the centre of scientific studies and trade, and many merchants and scientists from as far as China and India traveled there, as did al-Khwārizmī<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. He worked in the House of Wisdom established by the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Caliph al-Ma'mūn</a>, where he studied the sciences and mathematics, including the translation of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> scientific manuscripts.
</p><p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Al-Wathiq" title="Al-Wathiq">al-Wathiq</a>, he is said to have been involved in the first of two embassies to the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Douglas_Morton_Dunlop" title="Douglas Morton Dunlop">Douglas Morton Dunlop</a> suggests that Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī might have been the same person as Muḥammad ibn Mūsā ibn Shākir, the eldest of the three <a href="/wiki/Ban%C5%AB_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Banū Mūsā">Banū Mūsā</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions">Contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Contributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg/220px-Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Image-Al-Kit%C4%81b_al-mu%E1%B8%ABta%E1%B9%A3ar_f%C4%AB_%E1%B8%A5is%C4%81b_al-%C4%9Fabr_wa-l-muq%C4%81bala.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from al-Khwārizmī's <i>Algebra</i></div></div></div>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī's contributions to mathematics, geography, astronomy, and <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> established the basis for innovation in algebra and <a href="/wiki/Trigonometry" title="Trigonometry">trigonometry</a>. His systematic approach to solving linear and quadratic equations led to <i>algebra</i>, a word derived from the title of his book on the subject, "The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>On the Calculation with Hindu Numerals,</i> written about 820, was principally responsible for spreading the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> throughout the Middle East and Europe. It was translated into Latin as <i>Algoritmi de numero Indorum</i>. Al-Khwārizmī, rendered as (Latin) <i>Algoritmi</i>, led to the term "algorithm".
</p><p>Some of his work was based on Persian and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> astronomy, <a href="/wiki/Indian_numbering_system" title="Indian numbering system">Indian numbers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematics</a>.
</p><p>Al-Khwārizmī systematized and corrected <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s data for Africa and the Middle East. Another major book was <i>Kitab surat al-ard</i> ("The Image of the Earth"; translated as Geography), presenting the coordinates of places based on those in the <a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)"><i>Geography</i> of Ptolemy</a> but with improved values for the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, Asia, and Africa.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>He also wrote on mechanical devices like the <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sundial" title="Sundial">sundial</a>.  He assisted a project to determine the circumference of the Earth and in making a world map for <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">al-Ma'mun</a>, the caliph, overseeing 70 geographers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> When, in the 12th century, his works spread to Europe through Latin translations, it had a profound impact on the advance of mathematics in Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algebra">Algebra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Algebra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Compendious_Book_on_Calculation_by_Completion_and_Balancing" title="The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing">The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">Latin translations of the 12th century</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">Mathematics in medieval Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Science in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:258px;max-width:258px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_(Arabic).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="369" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/130px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/195px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png/260px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28Arabic%29.png 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_(English).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="390" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/120px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/180px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png/240px-The_Algebra_of_Mohammed_ben_Musa_%28English%29.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: The original Arabic print manuscript of the <i>Book of Algebra</i> by Al-Khwārizmī. Right: A page from <i>The Algebra of Al-Khwarizmi</i> by Fredrick Rosen, in English.</div></div></div></div>
<p><i>The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الكتاب المختصر في حساب الجبر والمقابلة</span> <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-Kitāb al-mukhtaṣar fī ḥisāb al-jabr wal-muqābala</i></span>) is a mathematical book written approximately 820 CE. The book was written with the encouragement of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Caliph al-Ma'mun</a> as a popular work on calculation and is replete with examples and applications to a wide range of problems in trade, surveying and legal inheritance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen-43">[42]</a></sup> The term "algebra" is derived from the name of one of the basic operations with equations (<span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-jabr</i></span>, meaning "restoration", referring to adding a number to both sides of the equation to consolidate or cancel terms) described in this book. The book was translated in Latin as <i>Liber algebrae et almucabala</i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Chester" title="Robert of Chester">Robert of Chester</a> (<a href="/wiki/Segovia" title="Segovia">Segovia</a>, 1145) hence "algebra", and also by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_of_Cremona" title="Gerard of Cremona">Gerard of Cremona</a>. A unique Arabic copy is kept at Oxford and was translated in 1831 by F. Rosen. A Latin translation is kept in Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>It provided an exhaustive account of solving polynomial equations up to the second degree,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage228_228]_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyer1991[httpsarchiveorgdetailshistoryofmathema00boyepage228_228]-45">[44]</a></sup> and discussed the fundamental methods of "reduction" and "balancing", referring to the transposition of terms to the other side of an equation, that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyer-229_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boyer-229-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Khwārizmī's method of solving linear and quadratic equations worked by first reducing the equation to one of six standard forms (where <i>b</i> and <i>c</i> are positive integers)
</p>
<ul><li>squares equal roots (<i>ax</i><sup>2</sup> = <i>bx</i>)</li>
<li>squares equal number (<i>ax</i><sup>2</sup> = <i>c</i>)</li>
<li>roots equal number (<i>bx</i> = <i>c</i>)</li>
<li>squares and roots equal number (<i>ax</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>bx</i> = <i>c</i>)</li>
<li>squares and number equal roots (<i>ax</i><sup>2</sup> + <i>c</i> = <i>bx</i>)</li>
<li>roots and number equal squares (<i>bx</i> + <i>c</i> = <i>ax</i><sup>2</sup>)</li></ul>
<p>by dividing out the coefficient of the square and using the two operations <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-jabr</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الجبر</span> "restoring" or "completion") and <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-muqābala</i></span> ("balancing"). <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Al-jabr</i></span> is the process of removing negative units, roots and squares from the equation by adding the same quantity to each side. For example, <i>x</i><sup>2</sup> = 40<i>x</i> − 4<i>x</i><sup>2</sup> is reduced to 5<i>x</i><sup>2</sup> = 40<i>x</i>. <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Al-muqābala</i></span> is the process of bringing quantities of the same type to the same side of the equation. For example, <i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + 14 = <i>x</i> + 5 is reduced to <i>x</i><sup>2</sup> + 9 = <i>x</i>.
</p><p>The above discussion uses modern mathematical notation for the types of problems that the book discusses. However, in al-Khwārizmī's day, most of this notation <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematical_notation" title="History of mathematical notation">had not yet been invented</a>, so he had to use ordinary text to present problems and their solutions. For
example, for one problem he writes, (from an 1831 translation)
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If some one says: "You divide ten into two parts: multiply the one by itself; it will be equal to the other taken eighty-one times." Computation: You say, ten less a thing, multiplied by itself, is a hundred plus a square less twenty things, and this is equal to eighty-one things. Separate the twenty things from a hundred and a square, and add them to eighty-one. It will then be a hundred plus a square, which is equal to a hundred and one roots. Halve the roots; the moiety is fifty and a half. Multiply this by itself, it is two thousand five hundred and fifty and a quarter. Subtract from this one hundred; the remainder is two thousand four hundred and fifty and a quarter. Extract the root from this; it is forty-nine and a half. Subtract this from the moiety of the roots, which is fifty and a half. There remains one, and this is one of the two parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Algebra_1831_translation_rosen-43">[42]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In modern notation this process, with <i>x</i> the "thing" (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">شيء</span></span> <i>shayʾ</i>) or "root", is given by the steps,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle (10-x)^{2}=81x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>x</mi>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>

<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="(10-x)^{2}=81x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/333829401ca5524169bffb059def773ef0fcd4bd" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.112ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle 100+x^{2}-20x=81x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>Several authors have also published texts under the name of <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb al-jabr wal-muqābala</i></span>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_%E1%B8%A4an%C4%ABfa_D%C4%ABnawar%C4%AB" title="Abū Ḥanīfa Dīnawarī">Abū Ḥanīfa Dīnawarī</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_K%C4%81mil_Shuj%C4%81%CA%BF_ibn_Aslam" title="Abū Kāmil Shujāʿ ibn Aslam">Abū Kāmil Shujāʿ ibn Aslam</a>, Abū Muḥammad al-'Adlī, Abū Yūsuf al-Miṣṣīṣī, <a href="/wiki/%27Abd_al-Ham%C4%ABd_ibn_Turk" title="'Abd al-Hamīd ibn Turk">'Abd al-Hamīd ibn Turk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sind_ibn_Ali" title="Sind ibn Ali">Sind ibn 'Alī</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sahl_ibn_Bishr" title="Sahl ibn Bishr">Sahl ibn Bišr</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharaf_al-D%C4%ABn_al-%E1%B9%AC%C5%ABs%C4%AB" title="Sharaf al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī">Sharaf al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī</a>.
</p><p>S. Gandz has described Al-Khwarizmi as the father of Algebra :
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Al-Khwarizmi's algebra is regarded as the foundation and cornerstone of the sciences. In a sense, al-Khwarizmi is more entitled to be called "the father of algebra" than Diophantus because al-Khwarizmi is the first to teach algebra in an elementary form and for its own sake, Diophantus is primarily concerned with the theory of numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Victor J. Katz adds :
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The first true algebra text which is still extant is the work on al-jabr and al-muqabala by Mohammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi, written in Baghdad around 825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>J.J. O'Conner and E.F. Robertson wrote in the <i><a href="/wiki/MacTutor_History_of_Mathematics_archive" title="MacTutor History of Mathematics archive">MacTutor History of Mathematics archive</a></i>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Perhaps one of the most significant advances made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematics_in_medieval_Islam" title="Mathematics in medieval Islam">Arabic mathematics</a> began at this time with the work of al-Khwarizmi, namely the beginnings of algebra. It is important to understand just how significant this new idea was. It was a revolutionary move away from the Greek concept of mathematics which was essentially geometry. Algebra was a unifying theory which allowed <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irrational_number" title="Irrational number">irrational numbers</a>, geometrical magnitudes, etc., to all be treated as "algebraic objects". It gave mathematics a whole new development path so much broader in concept to that which had existed before, and provided a vehicle for future development of the subject. Another important aspect of the introduction of algebraic ideas was that it allowed mathematics to be applied to itself in a way which had not happened before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-49">[48]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>R. Rashed and Angela Armstrong write:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Al-Khwarizmi's text can be seen to be distinct not only from the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics" title="Babylonian mathematics">Babylonian tablets</a>, but also from <a href="/wiki/Diophantus" title="Diophantus">Diophantus</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Arithmetica" title="Arithmetica">Arithmetica</a></i>. It no longer concerns a series of <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problems to be solved</a>, but an <a href="/wiki/Rhetorical_modes#Exposition" title="Rhetorical modes">exposition</a> which starts with primitive terms in which the combinations must give all possible prototypes for equations, which henceforward explicitly constitute the true object of study. On the other hand, the idea of an equation for its own sake appears from the beginning and, one could say, in a generic manner, insofar as it does not simply emerge in the course of solving a problem, but is specifically called on to define an infinite class of problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>
According to Swiss-American historian of mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Florian_Cajori" title="Florian Cajori">Florian Cajori</a>, Al-Khwarizmi's algebra was different from the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_mathematicians" title="Indian mathematicians">Indian mathematicians</a>, for Indians had no rules like the ''restoration'' and ''reduction''.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Regarding the dissimilarity and significance of Al-Khwarizmi's algebraic work from that of Indian Mathematician <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Benjamin_Boyer" title="Carl Benjamin Boyer">Carl Benjamin Boyer</a> wrote: </p><blockquote><p>It is true that in two respects the work of al-Khowarizmi represented a retrogression from that of Diophantus. First, it is on a far more elementary level than that found in the Diophantine problems and, second, the algebra of al-Khowarizmi is thoroughly rhetorical, with none of the syncopation found in the Greek <i>Arithmetica</i> or in Brahmagupta's work. Even numbers were written out in words rather than symbols! It is quite unlikely that al-Khwarizmi knew of the work of Diophantus, but he must have been familiar with at least the astronomical and computational portions of Brahmagupta; yet neither al-Khwarizmi nor other Arabic scholars made use of syncopation or of negative numbers. Nevertheless, the <i>Al-jabr</i> comes closer to the elementary algebra of today than the works of either Diophantus or Brahmagupta, because the book is not concerned with difficult problems in indeterminant analysis but with a straight forward and elementary exposition of the solution of equations, especially that of second degree. The Arabs in general loved a good clear argument from premise to conclusion, as well as systematic organization – respects in which neither Diophantus nor the Hindus excelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p></blockquote><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dixit_algorizmi.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="1001" decoding="async" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/220px-Dixit_algorizmi.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/330px-Dixit_algorizmi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dixit_algorizmi.png/440px-Dixit_algorizmi.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dixit_algorizmi.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from a Latin translation, beginning with "Dixit algorizmi"</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic">Arithmetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Arithmetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gregor_Reisch,_Margarita_Philosophica,_1508_(1230x1615).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1591" data-file-width="1205" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png/220px-Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png/330px-Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png/440px-Gregor_Reisch%2C_Margarita_Philosophica%2C_1508_%281230x1615%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gregor_Reisch,_Margarita_Philosophica,_1508_(1230x1615).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Algorists vs. abacists, depicted in a sketch from 1508 CE</div></div></div>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī's second most influential work was on the subject of arithmetic, which survived in Latin translations but is lost in the original Arabic. His writings include the text <i>kitāb al-ḥisāb al-hindī</i> ('Book of Indian computation'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[note 2]</a></sup>), and perhaps a more elementary text, <i>kitab al-jam' wa'l-tafriq al-ḥisāb al-hindī</i> ('Addition and subtraction in Indian arithmetic').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnett201739_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnett201739-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> These texts described algorithms on decimal numbers (<a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numerals</a>) that could be carried out on a dust board. Called <i>takht</i> in Arabic (Latin: <i>tabula</i>), a board covered with a thin layer of dust or sand was employed for calculations, on which figures could be written with a stylus and easily erased and replaced when necessary. Al-Khwarizmi's algorithms were used for almost three centuries, until replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Uqlidisi" title="Al-Uqlidisi">Al-Uqlidisi</a>'s algorithms that could be carried out with pen and paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of 12th century wave of Arabic science flowing into Europe via translations, these texts proved to be revolutionary in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>  Al-Khwarizmi's <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latinised_names" title="List of Latinised names">Latinized</a> name, <i>Algorismus</i>, turned into the <a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">name of method</a> used for computations, and survives in the modern term "<a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a>". It gradually replaced the previous abacus-based methods used in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Four Latin texts providing adaptions of Al-Khwarizmi's methods have survived, even though none of them is believed to be a literal translation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnett201739_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnett201739-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Dixit Algorizmi</i> (published in 1857 under the title <i>Algoritmi de Numero Indorum</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Algorithmi_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Algorithmi-60">[58]</a></sup>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thus_spake_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thus_spake-61">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Liber Alchoarismi de Practica Arismetice</i></li>
<li><i>Liber Ysagogarum Alchorismi</i></li>
<li><i>Liber Pulveris</i></li></ul>
<p><i>Dixit Algorizmi</i> ('Thus spake Al-Khwarizmi') is the starting phrase of a manuscript in the University of Cambridge library, which is generally referred to by its 1857 title <i>Algoritmi de Numero Indorum</i>. It is attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Adelard_of_Bath" title="Adelard of Bath">Adelard of Bath</a>, who had also translated the astronomical tables in 1126. It is perhaps the closest to Al-Khwarizmi's own writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thus_spake_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thus_spake-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Khwarizmi's work on arithmetic was responsible for introducing the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93Arabic_numeral_system" title="Hindu–Arabic numeral system">Hindu–Arabic numeral system</a> developed in <a href="/wiki/Indian_mathematics" title="Indian mathematics">Indian mathematics</a>, to the Western world. The term "algorithm" is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Algorism" title="Algorism">algorism</a>, the technique of performing arithmetic with Hindu-Arabic numerals developed by al-Khwārizmī. Both "algorithm" and "algorism" are derived from the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latinised_names" title="List of Latinised names">Latinized forms</a> of al-Khwārizmī's name, <i>Algoritmi</i> and <i>Algorismi</i>, respectively.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world">Astronomy in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_(1).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1079" data-file-width="751" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/220px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/330px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png/440px-Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_%281%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corpus_Christ_College_MS_283_(1).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from <i>Corpus Christi College MS 283</i>. A Latin translation of al-Khwārizmī's <i>Zīj</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī's <a href="/wiki/Zij_al-Sindhind" title="Zij al-Sindhind"><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Zīj al-Sindhind</i></span></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-toomer-38">[37]</a></sup> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">زيج السند هند</span>, "<a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">astronomical tables</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Siddhanta#Astronomy" title="Siddhanta">Siddhanta</a></i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurston1996_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thurston1996-62">[60]</a></sup>) is a work consisting of approximately 37 chapters on calendrical and astronomical calculations and 116 tables with calendrical, astronomical and astrological data, as well as a table of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> values.  This is the first of many Arabic <i><a href="/wiki/Zij" title="Zij">Zijes</a></i> based on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_astronomy" title="Indian astronomy">Indian astronomical</a> methods known as the <i>sindhind</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-1956_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-1956-63">[61]</a></sup>  The word Sindhind is a corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>Siddhānta</i>, which is the usual designation of an astronomical textbook. In fact, the mean motions in the tables of al-Khwarizmi are derived from those in the "corrected Brahmasiddhanta" (<a href="/wiki/Br%C4%81hmasphu%E1%B9%ADasiddh%C4%81nta" title="Brāhmasphuṭasiddhānta">Brahmasphutasiddhanta</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Brahmagupta" title="Brahmagupta">Brahmagupta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The work contains tables for the movements of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a> and the five <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> known at the time. This work marked the turning point in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">Islamic astronomy</a>. Hitherto, Muslim astronomers had adopted a primarily research approach to the field, translating works of others and learning already discovered knowledge. 
</p><p>The original Arabic version (written c. 820) is lost, but a version by the Spanish astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maslamah_Ibn_Ahmad_al-Majriti" title="Maslamah Ibn Ahmad al-Majriti">Maslamah Ibn Ahmad al-Majriti</a> (c. 1000) has survived in a Latin translation, presumably by <a href="/wiki/Adelard_of_Bath" title="Adelard of Bath">Adelard of Bath</a> (26 January 1126).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> The four surviving manuscripts of the Latin translation are kept at the Bibliothèque publique (Chartres), the Bibliothèque Mazarine (Paris), the Biblioteca Nacional (Madrid) and the Bodleian Library (Oxford).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trigonometry">Trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī's <i>Zīj al-Sindhind</i> also contained tables for the <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_functions" title="Trigonometric functions">trigonometric functions</a> of sines and cosine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-1956_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-1956-63">[61]</a></sup> A related treatise on <a href="/wiki/Spherical_trigonometry" title="Spherical trigonometry">spherical trigonometry</a> is also attributed to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacTutor_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Khwārizmī produced accurate sine and cosine tables, and the first table of tangents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sesiano_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sesiano-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="268" data-file-width="371" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png/220px-Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png/330px-Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/08/Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Khwarizmi%27s_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hubert_Daunicht&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hubert Daunicht (page does not exist)">Daunicht</a>'s reconstruction of the section of al-Khwārizmī's world map concerning the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/220px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/330px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/PtolemyWorldMap.jpg/440px-PtolemyWorldMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PtolemyWorldMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_world_map" title="Ptolemy's world map">15th-century version</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Geography" title="Ptolemy's Geography"><i>Geography</i></a> for comparison.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile,_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1601" data-file-width="2134" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg/220px-Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg/330px-Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg/440px-Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile%2C_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earliest_extant_map_of_the_Nile,_in_al-Khw%C4%81razm%C4%AB%E2%80%99s_Kit%C4%81b_%E1%B9%A3%C5%ABrat_al-_ar%E1%B8%8D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest extant map of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, in al-Khwārazmī's Kitāb ṣūrat al- arḍ</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1983_CPA_5426_(1).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="985" data-file-width="735" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/220px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/330px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png/440px-1983_CPA_5426_%281%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1983_CPA_5426_(1).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp">stamp</a> issued 6 September 1983 in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, commemorating al-Khwārizmī's (approximate) 1200th birthday.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khiva,_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg/220px-Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg/330px-Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg/440px-Khiva%2C_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khiva,_Statue_of_al-Khwarizmi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Al-Khwārizmī in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī's third major work is his <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb Ṣūrat al-Arḍ</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">كتاب صورة الأرض</span>, "Book of the Description of the Earth"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> also known as his <i>Geography</i>, which was finished in 833. It is a major reworking of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s second-century <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographia_(Ptolemy)" title="Geographia (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i>, consisting of a list of 2402 coordinates of cities and other geographical features following a general introduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is only one surviving copy of <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb Ṣūrat al-Arḍ</i></span>, which is kept at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strasbourg_University_Library" title="Strasbourg University Library">Strasbourg University Library</a>. A Latin translation is kept at the <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Biblioteca Nacional de España">Biblioteca Nacional de España</a> in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The book opens with the list of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longitudes" title="Longitudes">longitudes</a>, in order of "weather zones", that is to say in blocks of latitudes and, in each <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> zone, by order of longitude. As <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gallez" title="Paul Gallez">Paul Gallez</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2011)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi#Dubious" title="Talk:Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> points out, this excellent system allows the deduction of many latitudes and longitudes where the only extant document is in such a bad condition as to make it practically illegible. Neither the Arabic copy nor the Latin translation include the map of the world itself; however, Hubert Daunicht was able to reconstruct the missing map from the list of coordinates. Daunicht read the latitudes and longitudes of the coastal points in the manuscript, or deduces them from the context where they were not legible. He transferred the points onto <a href="/wiki/Graph_paper" title="Graph paper">graph paper</a> and connected them with straight lines, obtaining an approximation of the coastline as it was on the original map. He then does the same for the rivers and towns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-Khwārizmī corrected Ptolemy's gross overestimate for the length of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-188_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-188-71">[69]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> to the eastern shores of the Mediterranean; Ptolemy overestimated it at 63 degrees of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, while al-Khwārizmī almost correctly estimated it at nearly 50 degrees of longitude. He "also depicted the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Oceans</a> as <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">open bodies of water</a>, not land-locked <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">seas</a> as Ptolemy had done."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Covington_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Covington-72">[70]</a></sup> Al-Khwārizmī's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">Prime Meridian</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Fortunate_Isles" title="Fortunate Isles">Fortunate Isles</a> was thus around 10° east of the line used by Marinus and Ptolemy. Most medieval Muslim gazetteers continued to use al-Khwārizmī's prime meridian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy-188_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-188-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_calendar">Jewish calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Jewish calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Al-Khwārizmī wrote several other works including a treatise on the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">Hebrew calendar</a>, titled <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Risāla fi istikhrāj ta'rīkh al-yahūd</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">رسالة في إستخراج تأريخ اليهود</span>, "Extraction of the Jewish Era"). It describes the <a href="/wiki/Metonic_cycle" title="Metonic cycle">Metonic cycle</a>, a 19-year intercalation cycle; the rules for determining on what day of the week the first day of the month <a href="/wiki/Tishrei" title="Tishrei">Tishrei</a> shall fall; calculates the interval between the <a href="/wiki/Anno_Mundi" title="Anno Mundi">Anno Mundi</a> or Jewish year and the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_era" title="Seleucid era">Seleucid era</a>; and gives rules for determining the mean longitude of the sun and the moon using the Hebrew calendar. Similar material is found in the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ray%E1%B8%A5%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī">Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toomer_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-toomer-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_works">Other works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">Ibn al-Nadim</a>'s <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb al-Fihrist</i></span>, an index of Arabic books, mentions al-Khwārizmī's <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb al-Taʾrīkh</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">كتاب التأريخ</span>), a book of annals. No direct manuscript survives; however, a copy had reached <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">Nusaybin</a> by the 11th century, where its <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan bishop</a>, Mar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elias_bar_Shinaya" title="Elias bar Shinaya">Elias bar Shinaya</a>, found it. Elias's chronicle quotes it from "the death of the Prophet" through to 169 AH, at which point Elias's text itself hits a lacuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several Arabic manuscripts in Berlin, Istanbul, Tashkent, Cairo and Paris contain further material that surely or with some probability comes from al-Khwārizmī. The Istanbul manuscript contains a paper on sundials; the <i>Fihrist</i> credits al-Khwārizmī with <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Kitāb ar-Rukhāma(t)</i></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">كتاب الرخامة</span>). Other papers, such as one on the determination of the direction of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, are on the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_astronomy" title="Spherical astronomy">spherical astronomy</a>.
</p><p>Two texts deserve special interest on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morning_width" title="Morning width">morning width</a> (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Ma'rifat sa'at al-mashriq fī kull balad</i></span>) and the determination of the <a href="/wiki/Azimuth" title="Azimuth">azimuth</a> from a height  (<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Ma'rifat al-samt min qibal al-irtifā'</i></span>). 
</p><p>He also wrote two books on using and constructing <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabes</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors">Honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div class="side-box-flex">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is some confusion in the literature on whether al-Khwārizmī's full name is <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ابو عبد الله محمد بن موسى الخوارزمي</span></span> <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Abū ʿAbdallāh Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</i></span> or <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ابو جعفر محمد بن موسی الخوارزمی</span></span> <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Abū Ja'far Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</i></span>. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> notes in his Prolegomena: "The first to write on this discipline [algebra] was Abu 'Abdallah al-Khuwarizmi. After him, there was Abu Kamil Shuja' b. Aslam. People followed in his steps."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In the introduction to his critical commentary on Robert of Chester's Latin translation of al-Khwārizmī's <i>Algebra</i>, L.C. Karpinski notes that Abū Ja'far Muḥammad ibn Mūsā refers to the eldest of the <a href="/wiki/Ban%C5%AB_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Banū Mūsā">Banū Mūsā</a> brothers. Karpinski notes in his review on (Ruska 1917) that in (Ruska 1918): "Ruska here inadvertently speaks of the author as Abū Ga'far M. b. M., instead of Abū Abdallah M. b. M." Donald Knuth writes it as <span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Abū 'Abd Allāh Muḥammad ibn Mūsā al-Khwārizmī</i></span> and quotes it as meaning "literally, 'Father of Abdullah, Mohammed, son of Moses, native of Khwārizm,'" citing previous work by Heinz Zemanek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some scholars translate the title <i>al-ḥisāb al-hindī</i> as "computation with Hindu numerals", but Arabic <i>Hindī</i> means 'Indian' rather than 'Hindu'. A. S. Saidan states that it should be understood as arithmetic done "in the Indian way", with Hindu-Arabic numerals, rather than as simply "Indian arithmetic". The Arab mathematicians incorporated their own innovations in their texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFWiesner-HanksEbreyBeckDavila2017"><a href="/wiki/Merry_Wiesner-Hanks" title="Merry Wiesner-Hanks">Wiesner-Hanks, Merry E.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Buckley_Ebrey" title="Patricia Buckley Ebrey">Ebrey, Patricia Buckley</a>; Beck, Roger B.; Davila, Jerry; Crowston, Clare Haru; <a href="/wiki/John_P._McKay" title="John P. Bedford/St. Martin's. p. 419. <q>Near the beginning of this period the Persian scholar al-Khwarizmi (d. ca. 850) harmonized Greek and Indian findings to produce astronomical tables that formed the basis for later Eastern and Western research.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+World+Societies&amp;rft.pages=419&amp;rft.edition=11th&amp;rft.pub=Bedford%2FSt.+Martin%27s&amp;rft.date=2017&amp;rft.aulast=Wiesner-Hanks&amp;rft.aufirst=Merry+E.&amp;rft.au=Ebrey%2C+Patricia+Buckley&amp;rft.au=Beck%2C+Roger+B.&amp;rft.au=Davila%2C+Jerry&amp;rft.au=Crowston%2C+Clare+Haru&amp;rft.au=McKay%2C+John+P.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad+ibn+Musa+al-Khwarizmi"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Maher-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Maher_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Maher_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Maher, P. (1998), "From Al-Jabr to Algebra", <i>Mathematics in School</i>, 27(4), 14–15.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Oaks-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Oaks_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Oaks, J. (2009), "Polynomials and Equations in Arabic Algebra", <i>Archive for History of Exact Sciences</i>, 63(2), 169–203.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">(Boyer 1991, "The Arabic Hegemony" p. 229) "It is not certain just what the terms al-jabr and muqabalah mean, but the usual interpretation is similar to that implied in the translation above. The word al-jabr presumably meant something like "restoration" or "completion" and seems to refer to the transposition of subtracted terms to the other side of an equation; the word muqabalah is said to refer to "reduction" or "balancing" – that is, the cancellation of like terms on opposite sides of the equation."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boyer, Carl B., 1985. <i>A History of Mathematics</i>, p. 252. Princeton University Press. "Diophantus sometimes is called the father of algebra, but this title more appropriately belongs to al-Khowarizmi..." , "...the Al-jabr comes closer to the elementary algebra of today than the works of either Diophantus or Brahmagupta..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">S Gandz, The sources of al-Khwarizmi's algebra, Osiris, i (1936), 263–277, "Al-Khwarizmi's algebra is regarded as the foundation and cornerstone of the sciences. In a sense, al-Khwarizmi is more entitled to be called "the father of algebra" than Diophantus because al-Khwarizmi is the first to teach algebra in an elementary form and for its own sake, Diophantus is primarily concerned with the theory of numbers."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKatz">Katz, Victor J. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190327085930/https://eclass.uoa.gr/modules/document/file.php/MATH104/20010-11/HistoryOfAlgebra.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Stages in the History of Algebra with Implications for Teaching"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>VICTOR J.KATZ, University of the District of Columbia Washington DC, USA</i>: 190. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 October</span> 2017</span> – via University of the District of Columbia Washington DC, USA. <q>The first true algebra text which is still extant is the work on al-jabr and al-muqabala by Mohammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi, written in Baghdad around 825.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=VICTOR+J.KATZ%2C+University+of+the+District+of+Columbia+Washington+DC%2C+USA&amp;rft.atitle=Stages+in+the+History+of+Algebra+with+Implications+for+Teaching&amp;rft.pages=190&amp;rft.aulast=Katz&amp;rft.aufirst=Victor+J.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Feclass.uoa.gr%2Fmodules%2Fdocument%2Ffile.php%2FMATH104%2F20010-11%2FHistoryOfAlgebra.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad+ibn+Musa+al-Khwarizmi"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFFred_James_Hill,_Nicholas_Awde2003">Fred James Hill, Nicholas Awde (2003). <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/isbn_9780781810159" rel="nofollow"><i>A History of the Islamic World</i></a></span>. <li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKarpinski,_L.C.1912"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L._C._Karpinski" title="L.
